  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2000533767" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2000533767" />
    <a title="Alex Sarll" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5866774-alex-sarll"><img alt="Alex Sarll" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453246877p2/5866774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2000533767?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Sarll" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Sarll" href="/user/show/5866774-alex-sarll">Alex Sarll</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780224044240" href="/book/show/991641.Eleanor_of_Aquitaine">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2000533767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13382880626550687657">Eleanor of Aquitaine was one of history’s most formidable women, so inevitably many have had axes to grind regarding her, whether about the immorality and awfulness of her terrible acts and attitudes, or about the ahead-of-her-time awesomeness indicated by those exact same acts and attitudes. And then of course you have the school of historians desperate to prove that everyone throughout history has been just as dull as themselves, and anything interesting must have been made up. Weir tries to s</span>
  <span id="freeText13382880626550687657" style="display:none">Eleanor of Aquitaine was one of history’s most formidable women, so inevitably many have had axes to grind regarding her, whether about the immorality and awfulness of her terrible acts and attitudes, or about the ahead-of-her-time awesomeness indicated by those exact same acts and attitudes. And then of course you have the school of historians desperate to prove that everyone throughout history has been just as dull as themselves, and anything interesting must have been made up. Weir tries to steer a course between these thickets, concluding that we can't know for sure that Eleanor had affairs with her heroic uncle* Raymond of Poitiers, or her future husband's father Geoffrey of Anjou...but on the other hand, we can't prove that she didn't, and this wasn't just a case of that ludicrous old misogynist Bernard of Clairvaux getting his hair shirt in a twist. Similarly, while there's no conclusive evidence that Eleanor rode off to the clusterfuck that was the Second Crusade as head of a female band attired as Amazons, it does seem to have been a widespread notion that she did from a time not long after the events… Alas, Weir does draw the line at the Courts of Love, and seems noticeably less inclined to believe the romantic accounts when it comes to Henry II's mistress (and hence Eleanor’s rival) Rosamund. Most noticeable of all is Weir's reluctance to countenance any suggestion that relationships such as Richard’s with Philip of France, or Henry and Becket in their youth, had anything “unnatural” about them, just because they were devoted to each other and shared beds and little things like that. <br /><br />Aside from the lesser attention paid to women, even high-born ones, in the 12th century, simple attrition over the intervening centuries mean that there are gaps in what we can know about Eleanor, and at times the book becomes more a history of events to which she was adjacent, and on which she may have had some influence, though we can’t entirely be sure. At its worst, this gives us a couple of sections of The Middle Ages For Beginners, which in places contradict specifics elsewhere in the book (we're told forks had yet to be invented when we've already seen them in use in Byzantium) or common sense (“all livestock were slaughtered in the autumn, there being no means of feeding them through the winter” - and the next generation of livestock comes from where, exactly?). More often, it’s a level up from that, but in places this can still be frustrating: when Eleanor isn’t doing anything much, imprisoned or retired, then by all means keep things moving with events elsewhere in the Angevin empire. But it’s frustrating to have only a couple of lines mentioning that Eleanor drew up a set of sea laws which have been the basis of French maritime law ever since, with no further details, and then go back to more details of interminable warring over the sodding Vexin. Not that this unpredictable level of detail only applies when the focus is on Eleanor, mind: consider the incident where emissaries came offering youngest son John the Kingdom of Jerusalem on a plate, only for his father Henry to turn them down "for various reasons”. You’d think that might bear elaboration, wouldn’t you? But none is forthcoming here.<br /><br />Still, in so far as it is the advertised life of Eleanor, this is a pretty good one. And what a life she led. Pious French monarch Louis VII was really not the best first husband for someone brought up in a troubadour-infused court, descended from a notorious libertine. That's even before you consider that, given the extent of Eleanor’s domains, and the weakness of the French monarchy, becoming Queen of France was very much marrying down for her. The passionate Angevins, on the other hand, whose tempers supported rumours of their diabolical descent, offered a much better match. And one of the strongest impressions with which this book left me is of Henry II - lusty, martial, quick to anger but not without a brain or a sense of justice – as pretty much the model of the mediaeval monarch. It seems no coincidence that it was during his reign that the notion of King Arthur begins to coalesce into something like the chivalric default we now know. And there are echoes of the rest of the myth in his inner circle: William the Marshal is one of the few historical knights I can picture earning a seat at the Round Table, and indeed later stories support identifying him with Lancelot in so far as they suggest he had an affair with Eleanor. <br /><br />Alas, Henry's virtues all curdled in his beastly clutch of legitimate sons - vainglorious arse Henry the Young King, brutal Richard, the truly horrible Geoffrey (think lesser Shakespearean malcontent), and John, who was just an all-round prick. Honestly, I have never been so sure that a historical figure would, if alive today, frequently excuse their godawful behaviour as ‘bantz’. Yet after they all (bar John) revolt against their father, they're largely pardoned - youthful high spirits, I shouldn't wonder – whereas their accomplice Eleanor remains a prisoner for a decade. Which could be accounted misogyny, though I can equally understand it being more shocking if your soulmate turns against you than when the kids have a tantrum, even if it does happen to be an armed tantrum in alliance with Scotland and France. Certainly, one gets the impression that Henry never altogether lost his affection for Eleanor, as also he could never entirely hate Thomas a Becket, despite the archbishopric turning the latter into a terrible prig (who, incidentally, during his dispute with his King went out of his way to ensure rapists got off as lightly as possible. Because securing Church privilege was far more important than justice, after all. He’d still be right at home in the modern Catholic organisation, even if in those days the victims were sometimes adult women). <br /><br />And with those archetypal pieces in play – the mighty but overfond matriarch, her noble but flawed husband, and their brood of absolute shits – the scene is set for the rest of the story. The military back-and-forth, the incessant betrayals, can get wearing in places, though whenever we get a little more detail on individual engagements they come marvellously alive. The politicking is more reliably entertaining/shocking: poor Alais, supposed to be marrying Richard but instead kept as a mistress by his father, who occasionally contemplates marrying her himself, or else passing her on to marry young John…it’s like a cross-Channel Deep South. And for some reason Alais' brother (the son of Eleanor’s ex, of course) seemed to get all offended by this. What a snowflake, eh? And what with all the warring, and the primitive medicine, our principal cast are picked off one by one, until poor Eleanor, who had a good innings by our own standards let alone those of her age, has been predeceased by eight of her ten children and clearly just can’t face any more of a life that was nasty, brutish and not even short. And all in all it was more a good read than not, but you still get a much better sense of her grandeur from watching Hepburn in <i>The Lion in Winter</i>.<br /><br />*Later, unspecified rumours circle around Eleanor and another of her uncles, at which point I did get a certain <i>South Park</i> song stuck in my head.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13382880626550687657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3365023290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3365023290" />
    <a title="Laura Edwards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31128717-laura-edwards"><img alt="Laura Edwards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591463722p2/31128717.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3365023290?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Edwards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Edwards" href="/user/show/31128717-laura-edwards">Laura Edwards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31128717-laura-edwards?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3365023290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6055887408378319487">The three star rating is not indicative of Alison Weir's skills as an historian and a writer. The problem is the difficulty of writing a biography on any woman from this time period.<br/><br/>Because of the dearth of information regarding any woman, even as public a figure as Eleanor of Aquitaine, any biography on a woman from the time period reads more like a biography on the man/men in her life. As a result, this book focuses more on Henry II and Richard I than Eleanor. It's also a wonderful book if the</span>
  <span id="freeText6055887408378319487" style="display:none">The three star rating is not indicative of Alison Weir's skills as an historian and a writer. The problem is the difficulty of writing a biography on any woman from this time period.<br /><br />Because of the dearth of information regarding any woman, even as public a figure as Eleanor of Aquitaine, any biography on a woman from the time period reads more like a biography on the man/men in her life. As a result, this book focuses more on Henry II and Richard I than Eleanor. It's also a wonderful book if the reader hopes for an overview of architecture and life in the 12th Century. <br /><br />For the vast majority of the book, I had trouble understanding the fascination with Eleanor of Aquitaine. She didn't really make much of a difference until reaching her sixties. Even then, her actions were well short of extraordinary. I find Queen Marguerite of France (wife of Louis IX) a much more interesting subject than Eleanor. My guess would be the fascination with Eleanor grew from legend over the years instead of the factual. At least in Ms. Weir's book most of the fantasy is dismissed and we get a better overview of the real Eleanor. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6055887408378319487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_254052140" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254052140" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7292740-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325558991p2/7292740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254052140?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/7292740-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781446449028" href="/book/show/17429710-eleanor-of-aquitaine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7292740-amy?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254052140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9670856274239352108">It’s probably not a good idea to approach a book of medieval history with high entertainment expectations. Life in twelfth-century Europe? A grim, relentless grind of war, disease, famine (or food so bad famine’s almost preferable) and unquestioning obedience to God, king, overlord, husband—even for the Queen of England and duchess of what constitutes most of modern-day France.<br/><br/>So my attitude going into Eleanor of Aquitaine—dread, fortified by strong coffee—was just about right. And wouldn’t you </span>
  <span id="freeText9670856274239352108" style="display:none">It’s probably not a good idea to approach a book of medieval history with high entertainment expectations. Life in twelfth-century Europe? A grim, relentless grind of war, disease, famine (or food so bad famine’s almost preferable) and unquestioning obedience to God, king, overlord, husband—even for the Queen of England and duchess of what constitutes most of modern-day France.<br /><br />So my attitude going into Eleanor of Aquitaine—dread, fortified by strong coffee—was just about right. And wouldn’t you know? The book turned out to be kind of a page-turner. Though textually dense, it’s under 400 pages (sans notes and sources) and pretty good about relating the known facts about a medieval woman circumventing some pretty formidable cultural obstacles to shape the history of the continent in a nice, tight narrative.<br /><br />Alison Weir didn’t have a lot of direct source material to work with. At that time, queens mattered for 1) their dowries, 2) their dynastic connections, and 3) their baby yields (Eleanor had nine children who survived infancy), and her name appeared on documents only when she was acting as regent for her invasion-happy husband, Richard II. So Weir primarily relies on contemporary accounts by sometimes-petty, often gossipy, intermittently reliable monks and priests—the only literate members of this society. She’s left to do some speculation and creative storytelling, which works for me. I’d just as soon not read the minutiae of military campaigns and royal rulings.<br /><br />Medieval history isn’t really my thing; I’m more of an early American gal. And while 18th century America may be another country, the things it cares about—individual liberty and self determination, reason, natural rights, etc.—are more or less the same things Westerners care about now. Twelfth century Europe, on the other hand, is another planet, a berserk intermediate zone between hell and purgatory with scant regard for human life and nothing resembling our current conception of justice. (It’s easy to see why this period is a favorite of fantasy and sci-fi writers.) Its ruling classes, including Eleanor’s two husbands and various crowned sons (and occasionally, her) vacillate between unchecked abuse of power—murder, plunder, ruinous taxation—and the donning of hair shirts. This was the age of the Crusades, and Eleanor was famous for accompanying her first husband, Louis VII to the Holy Land. A lot of the West’s current hypocrisies and hysterias concerning Muslims started here (remember when George W. inserted “crusade” into a post-9/11 speech?). And then, as now, the conflict is as rooted in limited land and resources as much as a surfeit of religious feeling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9670856274239352108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_710251805" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/710251805" />
    <a title="Louise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9281934-louise"><img alt="Louise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336194494p2/9281934.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/710251805?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louise" href="/user/show/9281934-louise">Louise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=british-bio-hist">british-bio-hist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=france-bio-hist">france-bio-hist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer710251805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14202725675781310937">This is a very well researched narrative on the life of Eleanor of Aquitaine. Unlike other women of her era, she controlled property, was able to retain it and, therefore, was a participant in and not a bystander to events. The nuns of Fontevrault extol the "brilliance" of the "royal progeny" with which she "illuminated the world". There were no newspapers then, and being cloistered, they had only Eleanor's word on this. While she is the mother of 2 kings (3 if the "Younger King" counts), these </span>
  <span id="freeText14202725675781310937" style="display:none">This is a very well researched narrative on the life of Eleanor of Aquitaine. Unlike other women of her era, she controlled property, was able to retain it and, therefore, was a participant in and not a bystander to events. The nuns of Fontevrault extol the "brilliance" of the "royal progeny" with which she "illuminated the world". There were no newspapers then, and being cloistered, they had only Eleanor's word on this. While she is the mother of 2 kings (3 if the "Younger King" counts), these sons are not exactly distinguished. They cannot inspire their nobles;u they make war on each other and one of them essentially loses England's continental empire.<br /><br />Weir doesn't speculate on the daily prodding Eleanor might have given her sons to make war on their father, but she presents evidence to demonstrate that she tried to temper their wars against each other. Her efforts on behalf of the kidnapped Richard are extraordinary. There is no record, but legend has it that she sought leniency for her imprisoned (by her son) grandson and other legends have it that she killed him.<br /><br />I found it interesting how this family spends Christmas or Easter together... after and sometimes in the middle of all this drama. Another thing of interest in this family is the role of guilt. Henry II wears a hair shirt and orders himself lashed in the presence of witnesses including his family, Richard publically decries his unspeakable sins and Eleanor's writes the Pope that her sins have caused the troubles that have befallen Richard.<br /><br />Eleanor was in no way an ornament or passive queen. She managed kingdoms and rode along on 2 Crusades. It appears she engineered her divorce from the King of France, an accomplishment in its own right (witness the trouble Henry VIII had in this regard) and maintained some sort of useful relations with him throughout his life. She was imprisoned for 10(?) years, and came back without missing a beat.<br /><br />Weir conveys all this, drawing a picture of the times, what they wore, what they ate, how they traveled, the appearance of the castles and how they were defended and much more. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14202725675781310937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_710251805'><a id="like_count_review_710251805" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/710251805?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F111220.Eleanor_of_Aquitaine">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_884186464" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/884186464" />
    <a title="Lisa Feld" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3407733-lisa-feld"><img alt="Lisa Feld" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442510135p2/3407733.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/884186464?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa Feld" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa Feld" href="/user/show/3407733-lisa-feld">Lisa Feld</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3407733-lisa-feld?shelf=boston">boston</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer884186464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14392770964906909032">Weir faces some interesting challenges as a historian here: sifting reliable sources about Eleanor from apocryphal stories and putting together a coherent picture of the powerful queen from the traces left in chronicles about the men she influenced. What emerges is a remarkably clear and compelling story of an intelligent and capable woman who married two kings, birthed ten children, marched in two Crusades, ran kingdoms in her husbands' and sons' absences, incited her sons to rebellion, argued </span>
  <span id="freeText14392770964906909032" style="display:none">Weir faces some interesting challenges as a historian here: sifting reliable sources about Eleanor from apocryphal stories and putting together a coherent picture of the powerful queen from the traces left in chronicles about the men she influenced. What emerges is a remarkably clear and compelling story of an intelligent and capable woman who married two kings, birthed ten children, marched in two Crusades, ran kingdoms in her husbands' and sons' absences, incited her sons to rebellion, argued with the Pope to help free her son Richard from captivity, and remained a serious power broker into her late seventies.<br /><br />I particularly appreciated the ways Weir disagreed with previous historians by talking about which records were written at the time and which were created or embellished a century later, and the known biases of different chroniclers. Unlike with other historians of this period, I never felt she was glossing over or sidestepping issues or theorizing without evidence; whenever there is contradictory evidence or uncomfortable truths, she examines both sides and lays out her reasoning beautifully. And in talking about Eleanor's various husbands and children, Weir made sense of things I'd never understood. For example, I'd obviously heard the rumors that Richard the Lionheart had no heir because he was gay. But Weir pointed out that even if he might have been bisexual, he did have a bastard (as well as a rather disturbing record as a rapist) and she made the heir situation clearer: Richard was betrothed from childhood, but his father didn't let him marry (and was in fact sleeping with Richard's fiancée and had a child with her). It was only the year he went on Crusade that he finally secured another bride; he had little opportunity to deal with the succession before he was captured.<br /><br />In all, this is a wonderful and engaging history not just of Eleanor but of the Plantagenets; one of the best histories of the period I've read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14392770964906909032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2773531978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2773531978" />
    <a title="Ashley Abate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2506137-ashley-abate"><img alt="Ashley Abate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330222819p2/2506137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2773531978?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley Abate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley Abate" href="/user/show/2506137-ashley-abate">Ashley Abate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2773531978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer206747928283058576">All the delightful drama of medieval Europe within the scope of Eleanor of Aquitaine’s life and reign(s). This book was more about her times than her ,specifically; she seemed to cause a good deal of bedlam/bloodshed as a young woman and finally became a “great” queen as an old lady. She married a king, betrayed him twice (if the account in Jerusalem is to be believed), married his arch-rival, and then eventually stirred up a rebellion against him, led by all his sons and her first husband. 🤨<br/><br/>I e</span>
  <span id="freeText206747928283058576" style="display:none">All the delightful drama of medieval Europe within the scope of Eleanor of Aquitaine’s life and reign(s). This book was more about her times than her ,specifically; she seemed to cause a good deal of bedlam/bloodshed as a young woman and finally became a “great” queen as an old lady. She married a king, betrayed him twice (if the account in Jerusalem is to be believed), married his arch-rival, and then eventually stirred up a rebellion against him, led by all his sons and her first husband. 🤨<br /><br />I enjoyed this primer on Henry II and his horrible, terrible, no-good sons (Eleanor certainly didn’t shine as a mother) but I have to take stars off for Weir’s annoying habit of making sweeping observations or stereotyping that didn’t make sense or contradicted what she had said elsewhere in the book. Her moral judgements on King Henry II were contradictory and imprecise (how exactly was he “oppressive” to the aquitanians but a miracle king to the English?), as were some of her statements on Richard, domestic life in general, the Church, and the role of women in medieval <br /><br />The way she treated Eleanor’s imprisonment was completely RIDICULOUS. The woman had betrayed a king (straight up treason) for the third time in her short life and somehow kept her head on. I actually found that impressive. Weir acts like it was completely uncalled for for King Henry to keep her in confinement for her treason and like imprisonment was a horrible patriarchal punishment reserved for women. Hahahahaha. Like that doesn’t even begin to make sense, even in the context of her own story. <br /><br />Overall, this was really a sad life. Eleanor may have had some wonderful modern characteristics (power, wealth, semi-independence) but ultimately she and Henry reaped what they sowed: arrogant, selfish, and treasonous sons who broke their parents hearts and destroyed the kingdom they worked so hard to build. </span>
  <a data-text-id="206747928283058576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zR6Ws7EPPIHSZmrUjBZATldOk5aGYPmmqmX3f9jvc0jPT6hkD+mjnm0T4VFEr7Ida8gwl4Qx5rIbzyyi9Qrkuw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

David had known Jon and his twin brother since they were boys. He had coached them on the baseball field and welcomed them into his home for sleepovers with his own sons. However, when David learned how Jon\'s body was found, he felt compelled to find the facts behind this incomprehensible tragedy.\n\nSoon, David would learn of a brutal incident at a local motel where Jon and his brother had been severely beaten by police officers, the charges filed against those officers, and the months of harassment and intimidation Jon and his brother endured. Few in the utopian community of Columbia, Maryland, believed Jon could commit such a final act. Like many others, David wondered how a fateful night of teens blowing off steam could lead to such a tragic end. As law enforcement failed to find answers and seemed intent on preventing the truth from surfacing, David uncovered a system of cover-ups that could only lead to one conclusion--Jon\'s death was an In 1774, at the age of seventeen, Georgiana achieved immediate celebrity by marrying one of England\'s richest and most influential aristocrats, the Duke of Devonshire. Launched into a world of wealth and power, she quickly became the queen of fashionable society, adored by the Prince of Wales, a dear friend of Marie-Antoinette, and leader of the most important salon of her time. Not content with the role of society hostess, she used her connections to enter politics, eventually becoming more influential than most of the men who held office. Her good works and social exploits made her loved by the multitudes, but Georgiana\'s public success, like Diana\'s, concealed a personal life that was fraught with suffering. The Duke of Devonshire was unimpressed by his wife\'s legendary charms, preferring instead those of her closest friend, a woman with whom Georgiana herself was rumored to be on intimate terms. For over twenty years, the three lived together in a jealous and uneasy ménage à trois, during which time both women bore the Duke\'s children—as well as those of other men.Foreman\'s descriptions of Georgiana\'s uncontrollable gambling, all-night drinking, drug taking, and love affairs with the leading politicians of the day give us fascinating insight into the lives of the British aristocracy in the era of the madness of King George III, the American and French revolutions, and the defeat of Napoleon. A gifted young historian whom critics are already likening to Antonia Fraser, Amanda Foreman draws on a wealth of fresh research and writes colorfully and penetratingly about the fascinating Georgiana, whose struggle against her own weaknesses, whose great beauty and flamboyance, and whose determination to play a part in the affairs of the world make her a vibrant, astonishingly Africa’s Child is the story of a mixed-race girl growing up in the Usambara Mountains of Tanzania, East Africa. Raised in an orphanage with no knowledge of her origins or family, she endured abandonment, hardships, severe illnesses, and bullying. Her experiences as a child and teenager included physical, emotional, and sexual abuse, social stigma, and racial discrimination.Yet Nhambu tells her inspiring story with warmth and humor. Her questioning mind probes the African tribal realities and multi-cultural complexities that impacted her life both at the orphanage and schools run by German nuns as well as at an African high school with American nuns. Nhambu not only survived her childhood but triumphed. Her faith and resilience, along with a belief in learning and her tenacious pursuit of an education, sustained her through many challenges. Dance, especially African tribal dance, became the way she healed and nourished her spirit. Through the love and commitment of an American teacher she met in Africa, Nhambu was able to pursue her dream of education and a new life for herself. The first book in her three-part memoir ends as she is leaving Africa for university studies in America on a full scholarship.Maria Nhambu is the creator of Aerobics With Soul®, a fitness workout Though she carried her childhood self everywhere, Bobi also finally found her voice.\n\nAn elegiac account of survival despite being born poor, female, and cloistered, Bobi’s testament is one of hope for all vulnerable populations, particularly women and girls caught in the cycle of poverty and abuse. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/134958.Elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth: The Struggle for the Throne" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1402027586l/134958._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_134958'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/134958.Elizabeth?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Elizabeth: The Struggle for the Throne<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/78035.David_Starkey?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">David Starkey<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\" title=\"really liked it\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\">really liked it<\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 4.00 avg rating &mdash; 14,796 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2000\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8332124970171749846\">The legend of Elizabeth I, the untouchable, charismatic Virgin Queen, is a powerful and enduring one. Most biographies focus on the years of her reign, during which she proved herself as adept a ruler -- and as shrewd an operator -- as England had ev<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8332124970171749846\" style=\"display:none\">The legend of Elizabeth I, the untouchable, charismatic Virgin Queen, is a powerful and enduring one. Most biographies focus on the years of her reign, during which she proved herself as adept a ruler -- and as shrewd an operator -- as England had ever seen. But while the history of her rule is fascinating, the story of how her remarkable character was forged seems vital to a full understanding of the woman who led England into a new age of prosperity, power, and artistic achievement. David Starkey\'s Elizabeth: The Struggle for the Throne explores the terra incognita of Elizabeth\'s early years, and the result is nothing short of captivating.\n\nStarkey finds that Elizabeth\'s early years ran the gamut from days of snug security as the daughter of Henry VIII and Anne Boleyn, and Henry\'s heir apparent, to the years of uncertainty after her mother\'s execution when\nElizabeth was separated from court and virtually forgotten. She received a first-rate academic education, excelling in languages and rhetoric and exhibiting a strong interest in the Protestant religion her father had established in England. But the education she received from life itself would prove far more valuable for the monarch-to-be. After the death of Henry VIII and Edward VI (Elizabeth\'s half brother), Elizabeth\'s status as sister and would-be successor to the Catholic queen Mary put her in a dangerous position.\n\nIt also put her in prison at Mary\'s command -- and perilously close to execution -- after plots to place Elizabeth on the throne were revealed. Starkey makes it clear that while others may have actually done the dirty work, Elizabeth was usually in the thick of these efforts. Her imprisonment taught her to cover her tracks, but it did not stop her maneuverings. While fervently professing her Catholic faith, she surrounded herself with Protestant advisers and attendants, and bided her time. \n\nShe evaded another snare of her sister\'s when Mary attempted to neutralize Elizabeth by marrying her off to a Catholic Spaniard in exchange for naming Elizabeth her successor. Perhaps Elizabeth had learned early on from the plight of her mother that marriage had its drawbacks. Starkey, however, suggests that Elizabeth, in a moment of true regality, would not accept the crown if it came through bullying and capitulation. Again, Elizabeth bided her time.\n\nHer seeming patience -- for Starkey reveals that Elizabeth continued to plot -- paid off when Mary, never robust, entered her final illness. Elizabeth managed to convince the now irrational queen that she was, indeed, a staunch Catholic and vowed to preserve England as a Catholic realm. Upon Mary\'s death, Elizabeth ascended to the throne with no meaningful opposition, thereby beginning what would be a golden age, one of the most legendary reigns in history. Starkey lets us understand, for the first time, the forces that made her into the formidable woman -- and brilliant ruler -- that she would soon prove to be.<\/span>\n  <a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18144031-redefining-realness"><img alt="Redefining Realness: My Path to Womanhood, Identity, Love &amp; So Much More" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1394420737l/18144031._SX98_.jpg" />

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_18144031'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18144031-redefining-realness?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Redefining Realness: My Path to Womanhood, Identity, Love &amp; So Much More<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5388148.Janet_Mock?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Janet Mock<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.30 avg rating &mdash; 13,960 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2014\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8206504331039317572\">In 2011, Marie Claire magazine published a profile of Janet Mock in which she stepped forward for the first time as a trans woman. Those twenty-three hundred words were life-altering for the People.com editor, turning her into an influential and outs<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8206504331039317572\" style=\"display:none\">In 2011, Marie Claire magazine published a profile of Janet Mock in which she stepped forward for the first time as a trans woman. Those twenty-three hundred words were life-altering for the People.com editor, turning her into an influential and outspoken public figure and a desperately needed voice for an often voiceless community. In these pages, she offers a bold and inspiring perspective on being young, multicultural, economically challenged, and transgender in America.  Welcomed into the world as her parents’ firstborn son, Mock decided early on that she would be her own person—no matter what. She struggled as the smart, determined child in a deeply loving yet ill-equipped family that lacked the money, education, and resources necessary to help her thrive. Mock navigated her way through her teen years without parental guidance, but luckily, with the support of a few close friends and mentors, she emerged much stronger, ready to take on—and maybe even change—the world.  This powerful memoir follows Mock’s quest for identity, from an early, unwavering conviction about her gender to a turbulent adolescence in Honolulu that saw her transitioning during the tender years of high school, self-medicating with hormones at fifteen, and flying across the world alone for sex reassignment surgery at just eighteen. With unflinching honesty, Mock uses her own experience to impart vital insight about the unique challenges and vulnerabilities of trans youth and brave girls like herself.  Despite the hurdles, Mock received a scholarship to college and moved to New York City, where she earned a master’s degree, enjoyed the success of an enviable career, and told no one about her past. She remained deeply guarded until she fell for a man who called her the woman of his dreams. Love fortified her with the strength to finally tell her story, enabling her to embody the undeniable power of testimony and become a fierce advocate for a marginalized and misunderstood community. <span id="freeTextContainer11584096265971170159"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name.</i><br/><br/>Alison Weir is a British writer of history books for the general public, mostly in the form of biographies about British kings and queens, and of historical fiction. Before becoming an author, Weir worked as a teacher of children with special needs. She received her formal training in history at teacher training</span>
  <span id="freeText11584096265971170159" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name.</i><br /><br />Alison Weir is a British writer of history books for the general public, mostly in the form of biographies about British kings and queens, and of historical fiction. Before becoming an author, Weir worked as a teacher of children with special needs. She received her formal training in history at teacher training college. She currently lives in Surrey, England, with her two children.<br /></span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/977136-eleanor-of-aquitaine-a-life-world-leaders-past-present">Trivia About Eleanor of Aquita...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">No trivia or quizzes yet. <a href="/trivia/work/977136-eleanor-of-aquitaine-a-life-world-leaders-past-present">Add some now &raquo;</a></div>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>


      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/977136-eleanor-of-aquitaine-a-life-world-leaders-past-present">Quotes from Eleanor of Aquita...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;In this martial world dominated by men, women had little place. The Church's teachings might underpin feudal morality, yet when it came to the practicalities of life, a ruthless pragmatism often came into play. Kings and noblemen married for political advantage, and women rarely had any say in how they or their wealth were to be disposed in marriage. Kings would sell off heiresses and rich widows to the highest bidder, for political or territorial advantage, and those who resisted were heavily fined.<br /><br />Young girls of good birth were strictly reared, often in convents, and married off at fourteen or even earlier to suit their parents' or overlord's purposes. The betrothal of infants was not uncommon, despite the church's disapproval. It was a father's duty to bestow his daughters in marriage; if he was dead, his overlord or the King himself would act for him. Personal choice was rarely and issue.<br /><br />Upon marriage, a girl's property and rights became invested in her husband, to whom she owed absolute obedience. Every husband had the right to enforce this duty in whichever way he thought fit--as Eleanor was to find out to her cost. Wife-beating was common, although the Church did at this time attempt to restrict the length of the rod that a husband might use.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/898473-in-this-martial-world-dominated-by-men-women-had-little">11 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Court life for a queen of France at that time was, however, stultifyingly routine. Eleanor found that she was expected to be no more than a decorative asset to her husband, the mother of his heirs and the arbiter of good taste and modesty.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/898480-court-life-for-a-queen-of-france-at-that-time">9 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/977136-eleanor-of-aquitaine-a-life-world-leaders-past-present">More quotes&hellip;</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
